<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_5502').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_5502').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_5502').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

</script></div>

<div id="review_2519546451" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2519546451" />
    <a title="Daren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11995655-daren"><img alt="Daren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347678806p2/11995655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2519546451?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daren" href="/user/show/11995655-daren">Daren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385423243" href="/book/show/1193510.The_Harafish">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=africa-north">africa-north</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=egypt">egypt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=egyptian-author">egyptian-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2519546451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2156985098205626004">I was looking forward to this novel, having enjoyed immensely the Cairo Trilogy, the masterpiece by the same author. I looked forward to the sweeping narrative, the complex twists & turns of the generations of the al-Nagi family. However the depth with which Mahfouz wrote the Cairo Trilogy isn't present here. This is a much more superficial overview - it moves very quickly. Perhaps not less successful, but a very different model.<br/><br/>This book is separated into ten chapters or ten 'epics' as they are</span>
  <span id="freeText2156985098205626004" style="display:none">I was looking forward to this novel, having enjoyed immensely the Cairo Trilogy, the masterpiece by the same author. I looked forward to the sweeping narrative, the complex twists &amp; turns of the generations of the al-Nagi family. However the depth with which Mahfouz wrote the Cairo Trilogy isn't present here. This is a much more superficial overview - it moves very quickly. Perhaps not less successful, but a very different model.<br /><br />This book is separated into ten chapters or ten 'epics' as they are described. The first tells of the life of Ashur, the originator of the family line. Found on the street, raised in a poor family, he grows from humble origins to become a great leader. <br /><br />In each successive 'epic' we move down the generations - sometimes one, but more often two or three. Each 'epic' chronicles a new principal character. None are the same, many share character strengths, more share flaws, because this is not an uplifting, happy novel!<br /><br />To explain a few aspects a little more for background - events all take place one alley - presumably in Cairo. For the people of the alley - the common people (known as harafish*) and the well-to-do notables, the alley is their place, and the alley is led by a chief. Before Ashur, the chiefs were generally rogues who taxed the harafish, and were paid off by the notables. The chief quickly becomes overly greedy and is is soon conspired against by one of his henchmen, who looks to replace him with brute force. <br /><br />*There is a translators note in the book which says "The historical meaning of <i>harafish</i> is the rabble or the riffraff. In the novel it means the common people in a positive sense, those in menial jobs, casual workers, and the unemployed and homeless."<br /><br />As I alluded to above, this book rolls out through generations very quickly, often moving 20 years in a sentence. The Arabic names take a little bit of concentration for those unfamiliar with them, and there are some other family lines which also track from epic to epic. For me, while it was an interesting, fast paced novel, I missed the complex interactions and depth of story which were just not possible with the setup here. At times the characters seemed in a downwards spiral of morals and made futile attempts at honesty, but this story showed corruption and personal greed are far more common that any ability to act honestly for the greater good of a community. Should be obvious - modern politics show that.<br /><br />3.5 stars, rounded up.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2156985098205626004" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_228956419" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/228956419" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/228956419?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385423243" href="/book/show/1193510.The_Harafish">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=egypt">egypt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer228956419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4694524166613433062">Enjoyed this depiction of life in the "alley" over 10 generations of a family in Egypt, as various men, and occasional women, in a family struggle to become chief, or powers, of the alley or decide to (or are forced) live a "lower" life among the harafish, the common people. The tale is similar in some ways to the author's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5490.Children_of_the_Alley' title='Children of the Alley by Naguib Mahfouz' rel='nofollow noopener'>Children of the Alley</a>, being set in the same streets, and showing the rise and fall of men over years. But here, the writing is more lyrical, the ebb and flow of the family is</span>
  <span id="freeText4694524166613433062" style="display:none">Enjoyed this depiction of life in the "alley" over 10 generations of a family in Egypt, as various men, and occasional women, in a family struggle to become chief, or powers, of the alley or decide to (or are forced) live a "lower" life among the harafish, the common people. The tale is similar in some ways to the author's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5490.Children_of_the_Alley" title="Children of the Alley by Naguib Mahfouz" rel="nofollow noopener">Children of the Alley</a>, being set in the same streets, and showing the rise and fall of men over years. But here, the writing is more lyrical, the ebb and flow of the family is easier to follow, and there aren't the biblical (or Quranic) overtones.<br /><br />Mahfouz is a wonderful writer. Of course, there is no knowing how much to attribute to a translator. This struggle of men goes on today, trying to decide between helping the poor or reinforcing their own strength/wealth and the positions of family and friends. It seems to be an eternal struggle and is closely connected with corruption, weakness, poverty, violence, and multiple problems of all civilizations.<br /><br />Much to think about and see in our world. Not just a fable.<br /><br />A very strong 4</span>
  <a data-text-id="4694524166613433062" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer248090278" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17898286568287636715">Translated by Catherine Cobham. The sprawling saga of a Cairo family, the al-Nagis, starting with the first and greatest, who rose from a foundling into a benevolent clan chief who fought for the people (the harafish), and continuing for a dozen generations. After the first, however, the Nagis sink gradually into vice, oppression and madness; lust for power makes them rue the old days, while it precludes them from reconstructing them.<br/><br/>This is a confusing but rich novel. The number of minor charac</span>
  <span id="freeText17898286568287636715" style="display:none">Translated by Catherine Cobham. The sprawling saga of a Cairo family, the al-Nagis, starting with the first and greatest, who rose from a foundling into a benevolent clan chief who fought for the people (the harafish), and continuing for a dozen generations. After the first, however, the Nagis sink gradually into vice, oppression and madness; lust for power makes them rue the old days, while it precludes them from reconstructing them.<br /><br />This is a confusing but rich novel. The number of minor characters overwhelms you, but the major characters are vivid; Mahfouz shows great talent in creating so many discrete personalities. Parts of the novel are beyond subtle: ideas and plans are barely hinted at, so the reader must interpolate a lot. The end is rather startling – almost simplistic in its moral, and like a fairy tale in its complete resolution (the monastery doors, which have remained closed throughout the book, open when the final Nagi rouses the harafish to a kind of self-rule). I’m not sure what to make of that.</span>

          </span>

        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/304359-sara">Sara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109155961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4160197301621472396">This isn't actually one story, but rather a collection of 10 tales. I didn't like it because it was almost all exposition, description and getting from A to B. As soon as I was interested in a character and his or her story, it was quickly wrapped up to make way for the next tale, usually about the previous tale's character's child. It felt a lot like the book of Genesis: "A begat B, who married C and D and begat E, F and G..." I also didn't like that there was no sense of the passage of time or</span>
  <span id="freeText4160197301621472396" style="display:none">This isn't actually one story, but rather a collection of 10 tales. I didn't like it because it was almost all exposition, description and getting from A to B. As soon as I was interested in a character and his or her story, it was quickly wrapped up to make way for the next tale, usually about the previous tale's character's child. It felt a lot like the book of Genesis: "A begat B, who married C and D and begat E, F and G..." I also didn't like that there was no sense of the passage of time or of the outside world. I thought the first tale could have taken place in the early 1900s, but then there were at least several hundred more years worth of tales and nothing changed. A novel can be private, but this felt like it completely denied the effect that the world can have on a character - the tales were solely about the characters' births, deaths, marriages, children and quests for glory (which are limited to the alley that they all live in). I probably could have given it one star, but he does have a touch of poetry at times, and occasionally an interesting insight into humanity.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_198092414" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/198092414" />
    <a title="Tyler " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1096417-tyler"><img alt="Tyler " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377822301p2/1096417.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/198092414?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tyler " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tyler " href="/user/show/1096417-tyler">Tyler </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423359" href="/book/show/5502.The_Harafish">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Mahfouz; Fans of 20th Century Lit</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1096417-tyler?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer198092414" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer649544544743849338">The Harafish, which translates as “the common people,” traces an Egyptian family’s progress over ten generations in ten chapters. The point is to find out whether good character traits in a family can be handed down, or if they can even recur to the same effect, in later generations. This is a good idea for a book.<br/><br/>The number of characters in this epic means that readers gain in-depth knowledge of only a few. This is not a novel for people looking for character development. The plot has no time, </span>
  <span id="freeText649544544743849338" style="display:none">The Harafish, which translates as “the common people,” traces an Egyptian family’s progress over ten generations in ten chapters. The point is to find out whether good character traits in a family can be handed down, or if they can even recur to the same effect, in later generations. This is a good idea for a book.<br /><br />The number of characters in this epic means that readers gain in-depth knowledge of only a few. This is not a novel for people looking for character development. The plot has no time, either. About two centuries elapse, but the reader cannot place them as particular epochs in actual history. <br /><br />This narrative, then, is very much one of action and dialog, and the effect is to frame almost every relevant personality trait that might crop up in the context of a single family -- some people get rich, others are highly moral, some kill, some rape, at least one commits suicide, and, most important, many family members are conflicted or confused about what it takes to do the right thing. <br /><br />Other reviewers have called this a “lyrical” story, and in a way it is. The author uses repetition effectively, for example. But whatever lyrical quality the Arabic version has is inevitably lost in translation, and most of the the book reads like a straightforward storytelling. The translation is as good as can be expected, so even with the loss of lyricism in the English the book is still noticeably superior to most of what’s written in this language anyway. That’s a comment upon either the superiority of Arabic literature or the inferiority of English literature -- take your pick.<br /><br />For non-Egyptians this book sheds light on Egyptian social values, as do all of Mahfouz’s books. Apropos of current events, thugs are a leitmotif of the book, and in fact the Arab Spring has brought to the world’s attention the role thugs play in political repression. Certain other interesting and disturbing aspects of Egyptian social values also stand out to readers unfamiliar with Egypt.<br /><br />I give the book three stars and a good recommendation. My favorite Mahfouz novels are the ones written in the 1940’s, and this book comes from a later period in which the author experiments with structural elements of the novel. I breezed through it, but it didn’t grab me the way <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5499.Midaq_Alley" title="Midaq Alley by Naguib Mahfouz" rel="nofollow noopener">Midaq Alley</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7160.The_Beginning_and_the_End" title="The Beginning and the End by Naguib Mahfouz" rel="nofollow noopener">The Beginning and the End</a> did. Nevertheless, it is a Mahfouz, a thoughtful novel that entertains provocative ideas and leads to a quite surprising conclusion. People who have never read this author would enjoy the book, but it is better appreciated by people who have already read his earlier works.</span>
  <a data-text-id="649544544743849338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_271323393" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/271323393" />
    <a title="Beth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4705693-beth"><img alt="Beth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1293401563p2/4705693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/271323393?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth" href="/user/show/4705693-beth">Beth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423359" href="/book/show/5502.The_Harafish">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4705693-beth?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4705693-beth?shelf=around-the-world-group">around-the-world-group</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer271323393" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12852115140901919597"><i>In the passionate dark of dawn, on the path between death and life, within view of the watchful stars and within earshot of the beautiful, obscure anthems, a voice told of the trials and joys promised to our alley. </i> In <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5502.The_Harafish' title='The Harafish by Naguib Mahfouz' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Harafish</a>, Nobelist <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5835922.Naguib_Mahfouz' title='Naguib Mahfouz' rel='nofollow noopener'>Naguib Mahfouz</a> spins out an epic tale of many generations of a single family living in an Egyptian city. The tale begins with an abandoned infant, taken in by a local man and raised as his own. The family is poor, but the child is raised with love and taught</span>
  <span id="freeText12852115140901919597" style="display:none"><i>In the passionate dark of dawn, on the path between death and life, within view of the watchful stars and within earshot of the beautiful, obscure anthems, a voice told of the trials and joys promised to our alley. </i> In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5502.The_Harafish" title="The Harafish by Naguib Mahfouz" rel="nofollow noopener">The Harafish</a>, Nobelist <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5835922.Naguib_Mahfouz" title="Naguib Mahfouz" rel="nofollow noopener">Naguib Mahfouz</a> spins out an epic tale of many generations of a single family living in an Egyptian city. The tale begins with an abandoned infant, taken in by a local man and raised as his own. The family is poor, but the child is raised with love and taught to value justice. Rivalry develops with the biological son of the adoptive family, and so a complex saga begins. The tale involves vast changes in family fortunes and family and local politics. There is romance, there is scandal, there are honorable battles, and there are murders. There are debates about morality, the nature of fate, economics, and social justice, but all of them are framed in a poetic language that carries the tale along. It took me just a couple days to get through this book, and I found I wanted to get back to it from the breaks I had to take to engage in the real world. According to the translator, "the harafish" literally translates as riffraff, but in this novel means the common people. Just as the novel is the tale of a family and its fortunes, so this is a tale of the fate of the harafish living in this little alley and whether that fate will ever be in their own hands. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12852115140901919597" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3268357235" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3268357235" />
    <a title="robin friedman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman"><img alt="robin friedman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571353109p2/22269025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3268357235?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="robin friedman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="robin friedman" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman">robin friedman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423359" href="/book/show/5502.The_Harafish">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3268357235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3858712870773376960">The Harafish<br/><br/>Naguib Mahfouz' 1977 novel, "The Harafish," is an intriguing mix of allegory and saga. The term "harafish" refers to the large underclass of poor and downtrodden in an Egyptian city, presumably Cairo. The novel focuses upon the sufferings and the fortunes of the harafish and upon the history of a particular family, the Al-Nagis, over the course of generations. The story has a timeless quality with the author giving few details that identify either a specific place or a specific time.</span>
  <span id="freeText3858712870773376960" style="display:none">The Harafish<br /><br />Naguib Mahfouz' 1977 novel, "The Harafish," is an intriguing mix of allegory and saga. The term "harafish" refers to the large underclass of poor and downtrodden in an Egyptian city, presumably Cairo. The novel focuses upon the sufferings and the fortunes of the harafish and upon the history of a particular family, the Al-Nagis, over the course of generations. The story has a timeless quality with the author giving few details that identify either a specific place or a specific time. Yet, the novel succeeds in bringing the characters to life as individuals and in creating a sense of place.<br /><br />The book is told in ten chapters, each recounting successive adventures that befall the Al-Nagis. Each chapter is subdivided into many short paragraphs. The story moves forward with simplicity but becomes increasingly complex as it unfolds.<br /><br />There is a great deal of indirection in the book. The reader learns slowly by seeing and not by being told. Thus, Mafouz never explicitly explains the "clan" system at the heart of the book but rather shows the reader how it works. The "clan" is the informal ruler of an "alley" or section of a town. It can be analogized to an American gang or to a crime syndicate but enjoys quasi-official status. It accepts "protection" money, wars with neighboring gangs, keeps a semblance of order in the alley, and is headed by an all-powerful chief. Some of the religious leaders of the community are closely allied with the clan. The "alley" includes not only the many poor people, but rich and successful individuals as well, called the "notables". Most, but not all of the clan leaders ally themselves with the notables while exploiting the harafish.<br /><br />The chief character of Mafouz' tale is Ashur al-Nagi, a foundling who ultimately rises to the position of clan chief. Although he marries a prostitute and appropriates property that is not his, Ashur becomes a legend in the alley as a result of his compassion, strength, and protection of the harafish. His son, Shams-al-Din continues, for the most part, in the path of Ashur, but the family then deteriorates and its worst traits come to the fore. Its members, men and women, descend into murder, corruption and licentiousness. They move in and out of positions of power and are forever haunted by the fame of their illustrious ancestor. At the end of the book, another Ashur arises and restores and enhances upon the family name.<br /><br />Mahfouz' story unfolds with detail and with a deep compassion for the poor and the weak. There is a sense of human frailty and of the overriding force of change. The story suggests several themes. First, there is the sense of decline, reminding me of charismatic figures who found religion or social movements which soon fall into torpor. The story opens with something of a golden age with heroic figures and deeds. As it progresses, human life slips into the mundane. I also found in the book the suggestion that people tend to look too much to the alleged glorious deeds of their ancestors and judge themselves and their own potentialities falsely in their light. Mid-way in the story, one of the characters is reproached because the al-Nagi's view themselves in light of their founder, Ashur, and not in light of what they themselves can do. At the end, there is a deepening of the story. The final al-Nagi we meet, also named Ashur is said to be greater than his forbearer because "the first Ashur had relied on his own strength, while [the second Ashur] had made the harafish into an invincible force". While the first Ashur had conquered the evils of slum life, the second Ashur had achieved an even greater conquest: "his victory over himself". The second Ashur achieves a moment of spiritual awakening at the end.<br /><br />This is a fine book, both in its description of places, characters and societies and in the meditation it offers on the human condition.<br /><br />Robin Friedman</span>
  <a data-text-id="3858712870773376960" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_484294331" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484294331" />
    <a title="Tasneemali" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4770664-tasneemali"><img alt="Tasneemali" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593996873p2/4770664.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/484294331?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tasneemali" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tasneemali" href="/user/show/4770664-tasneemali">Tasneemali</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4770664-tasneemali?shelf=favorites-everr">favorites-everr</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer484294331" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4629557337912816364">To start with I am in love with the novel.<br/><br/>The main theme of the novel is seeking and struggling for justice.<br/><br/>It’s written in a poetic way that touches your soul and filled with all these philosophical questions. This whole anatomy of the character and its psychological depth I think is Naguib’s brilliant and one of a kind style!<br/><br/>Secondly, the novel totally made me relive “Hundred Years of Solitude” .Naguib did write the novel after ten years from Gabriel but I’m not saying Naguib is repeating him </span>
  <span id="freeText4629557337912816364" style="display:none">To start with I am in love with the novel.<br /><br />The main theme of the novel is seeking and struggling for justice.<br /><br />It’s written in a poetic way that touches your soul and filled with all these philosophical questions. This whole anatomy of the character and its psychological depth I think is Naguib’s brilliant and one of a kind style!<br /><br />Secondly, the novel totally made me relive “Hundred Years of Solitude” .Naguib did write the novel after ten years from Gabriel but I’m not saying Naguib is repeating him it’s just all those long generations with their believes in myths and queer lifestyle and actions kind of reminded me with it .<br />I read stuff about the novel and its metaphors and the recreation of many of our Islamic History leaders. That this time he succeeded even more in making all the inspired passages and parts hidden within many layers only for the elite to understand, and not like in “Awlad Haritna” where critics attacked Naguib because of the obvious similarities to our prophets or even some of our great leaders in Islamic history.<br /><br />Many argued that him writing those religious verses in a foreign language Farsi or Turkish was intentional. He wanted to convey that the rightful unbiased leader isn’t necessarily someone who understands religions verses fully or recites Qur’an all night , but rather someone who’s emotionally involved with religion , does good by it in actions and who’s in peace with this state of grace that overwhelms him whenever hearing those verses. <br /><br />And while writing this part I recalled a favourite quote from a favourite book to me “A Thousand Splendid Suns”<br />“At times, he didn't understand the meaning of the Koran's words. But he said he liked the enhancing sounds the Arabic words made as they rolled off his tongue. He said they comforted him, eased his heart”<br /><br />*Philosophical Quotes that resonated with me while reading the novel:<br /><br />*جادت السَّماء ببركاتها على عاشور و سعِد به قلب الشيخ عفرة زيدان عاماً بعدعام بقدرما سخط على درويش شقيقه و ربيبه .لمَ يا ربِّي و قد نشآ في حظيرة واحدة؟<br /><br />*لقد عرف من حقائق الدنيا على يد درويش في ليال ما لم يعرفه طيلة عشرين عاماً في كنف الشيخ الطيب عفرة زيدان . الأشرار معلِّمون قساة و صادقون !<br />*الأذن لا تسمع إلاَّ ما ترغب في سماعه !<br />*هل ذقت الهزيمة في حياتك ؟<br />لا أظن ، و لكن أي هزيمة ؟ من عدوك ؟<br />هزيمة الإنسان نفسه ، رضاؤك بالدمار إذا شئت ..<br /><br />*القبوع في البيت مفسدة للروح،مثير للمخاوف،مهوِّل للأحزان.أمَّا الحركة بركة.المعاملة تجديد للدماء و بعث للشجاعة ... رحيق الحياة يجري في ريقه . التوكُّل على الله ينعش روحه . الأمل يخطر من جديد .الإلهام يفعم و جدانه!<br />*لا دائم إلا الحركة.هي الألم و السرور ،عندما تخضرّ من جديد الورقة،عندما تنبت الزهرة،عندما تنضج الثمرة،تُمحى من الذاكرة سفعة البرد وجلجلة الشتاء.<br /><br />*جعلت تقول أن الإنسان يصبح بطول العمر عادة محبوبة يتعذَّر تصور الدنيا بغيرها.<br />*إنَّ الإنسان يشقى بساعة انتظار فكيف إذا صارت الحياة مُفرغة إلاَّ من انتظار متواصل ؟<br /><br />*تساءلت لم يضحك الإنسان ؟ لم يرقص بالفوز ؟ لم يطمئن سادراً فوق العرش ؟و لم ينسى دوره الحقيقي في اللعبة ؟ ولم ينس نهايته المحتومة ؟<br /><br />*ربَّاه أيوجد آخرون في الدنيا ؟من قال إذاً أنَّ الدنيا خالية ؟ خالية من اللون و الحركة والصوت . خالية من الحقيقة . خالية من الحزن و الأسى والندم.إنَّه في الواقع متحرِّر . لا حب و لا حزن.ذهب العذاب إلى الأبد.حلَّ السلام !<br />*لا تُرى يا معلِّم جلال إلاَّ ذاهباً أو آئباً ، عمَّ تبحث ؟<br />فأجابه بازدراء :<br />أجد ما أبحث عنه و أبحث عمَّا أجد.<br /><br />*ماجدوى الحزن؟ما فائدة السرور؟ما مغزى القوَّة؟ما معنى الموت؟لماذا يوجد المستحيل؟<br />*و سأله أبوه ذات صباح :<br />الناس يتساءلون متي يتحقق العدل ؟<br />فابتسم جلال بامتعاض و تمتم متسائلاً :<br />ما أهمية ذلك ؟ <br />فقال عبد ربُّه بدهشة :<br />إنَّه كلّ شئ يا بني ..<br />فقال بازدراء : إنَّهم يموتون كلَّ يوم و هم مع ذلك راضون ! <br />الموت علينا حق ، أمّا القهر و الذلّ فبيدك محقهما !<br /><br />*ذلك الجد الذي أحبَّه و آمن بعهده .وعبد خيره وقوته.أليس هومثله حباً في الخير و امتلاكاً للقوة؟ ولكن ماذا فعل كلاهما بخيره و قوته ؟ أمَّ الجد فقد حدث على يديه المعجزة، و أمَّا هو فيتحدَّى المجهول كما تحدَّاه فائزمن قبل وكما يتحدَّاه ضياء اليوم،ولكنه يشق طريقاً آخر،و يتطلع إلى آفاق بعيدة.إنه يواجه المجهول و يصافحه و يرمي بنفسه في خضمه.كأنما كُتبت عليه المغامرة و المقامرة و ركوب المستحيل.إنَّه يحمل سَّرا عجيباً ، ينبذ الأمن و السلامة،ويعشق الموت وما وراءه !<br /><br />*ما دام يوجد خطأ فلا بد أن يوجد صواب.وإذا وجد الصواب مرَّة فيمكن أن يوجد مرَّة أخرى،وإذا كان قد انتكس بعد و جوده فيمكن أن نضمن له حياة لا تعرف انتكاسة.<br /><br />*و خرج ضياء من الصمت القصير قائلاً:<br />ما أطول الأيام !<br />ثمَّ وهو يضحك:<br />ما أقصر الأيَّام!<br /><br />* تمَّ له أعظم نصر ، و هونصره على نفسه ...<br />*الظلام مرَّة أخرى .. فيه يختفي المُرهق من ذاته ، ليغرق في ذاته !<br /><br />*كانت سعادته سماء تظهر في جنباتها قطع السحاب ، و أحياناً تركض حتَّى تخفي وجه الشمس و قد يدهمه في أعذب اللحظات قلق غامض فيفتر حماسه و يتساءل عمَّا يعنيه ذلك . <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4629557337912816364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16737453985105213525">I was originally introduced to this book in a non-western lit class and i had to do a paper on it. I only read an excerpt and that seemed reasonably ok. I year later, my mom unknowingly bought it for me. The book was not really the type that i am used to reading but injoyed it.<br/>Its about generations of the Nagi family. It takes place in Cairo, Egypt. The book is seperated into short parts and each part is a generation from the Nagi family. The family starts off in glory and become legend. And as </span>
  <span id="freeText16737453985105213525" style="display:none">I was originally introduced to this book in a non-western lit class and i had to do a paper on it. I only read an excerpt and that seemed reasonably ok. I year later, my mom unknowingly bought it for me. The book was not really the type that i am used to reading but injoyed it.<br />Its about generations of the Nagi family. It takes place in Cairo, Egypt. The book is seperated into short parts and each part is a generation from the Nagi family. The family starts off in glory and become legend. And as the years go back, it seems that the desendants of the great Ashur Nagi have forgotten his code of honor and justice. So basically you have see if they eventually go back to their founding father's teachings and rule the 'harafish' or common ppl with justice.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8971790893516229694">J. k. Rowling has once said: "It's our choices that show who we really are, far more than our abilities."<br/>Naguib Mahfouz's brilliance is really obvious here. He basically summed up life with all it's perks and sin and karma. I don't think i'll ever get over his ability to write a novel of almost 600 pages discussing 10 generations. Each generation has it's own different story but somehow they're all connected. I tried to read it as slow as possible so i could feel everything and enjoy everything.</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32279091-maggie-ayau?shelf=2019-reading-challenge">2019-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2829752652" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12491270747841684442">I needed to read this quickly because I recently remembered that I’ve been borrowing it from a friend for over two years now. (Sorry Kyler.) <br/><br/>Another mark in my interest in reading storylines that diverge from a typical arc. This one is more like an actual line. The epic starts at the specific point of discovery of the newborn Ashur al-Nagi outside of a Cairo monestery. Ashur grows up to be a noble and just leader of the clan that rules the alley, respected by the harafish, or the working and low</span>
  <span id="freeText12491270747841684442" style="display:none">I needed to read this quickly because I recently remembered that I’ve been borrowing it from a friend for over two years now. (Sorry Kyler.) <br /><br />Another mark in my interest in reading storylines that diverge from a typical arc. This one is more like an actual line. The epic starts at the specific point of discovery of the newborn Ashur al-Nagi outside of a Cairo monestery. Ashur grows up to be a noble and just leader of the clan that rules the alley, respected by the harafish, or the working and lower classes (translated as “rabble” or “riffraff”, e.g. commoners, from the original Egyptian Arabic). We see the Nagi line unfold over hundreds of years, with each new generation struggling with power, morality, and familial duty. As time stretches on, the fabled Ashur and other early patriarchs (Shams al-Din, Sulayman) evolve more and more into figures of legend, with Ashur eventually becoming the venerated prophet-hero of the Nagi dynasty. Kind of like King Arthur, Aeneas, Abraham, Godric Gryffindor, or Isildur, High King of Gondor.<br /><br />There are some LOVELY, TRULY LOVELY poetic moments and phrases here.<br /><br />Enjoyed the structure. The epic is split into ten tales, which each tale focusing on a different set of descendents and consisting of 50-70 vignettes or sagas. <br /><br />Lots of turnover character-wise. New passion project is making a family tree of the Nagi line. Sometimes I rushed through stories but they really demand to be read slowly and carefully.<br /><br />Besides the sense of clutter around the characters adds to the motion of the epic. The farther out from Ashur we travel with his descendents, the more their names and stories accumulate and jumble. We start to look for patterns to help us maintain the thread between them. Do certain traits get passed down from person to person? Is it the power itself a source of corruption, or is it only corruptible people who are drawn to power?<br /><br />Why did I picture JEFF GOLDBLUM AS AZIZ</span>
  <a data-text-id="12491270747841684442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer452383715505328405">Feels weird to review an Arabic book in English, but I feel like I wouldn't be able to give this book its fair dues otherwise. Having only recently gotten back to reading Arabic books, a thing I am woefully behind on, I am blown away by everything in this novel. The marriage of fantasy and reality, in a very specific Egyptian flavor that feels like I'm being told an age-old myth. The sensation of time being cyclical, generations of the same family going through the same events - or a variation o</span>
  <span id="freeText452383715505328405" style="display:none">Feels weird to review an Arabic book in English, but I feel like I wouldn't be able to give this book its fair dues otherwise. Having only recently gotten back to reading Arabic books, a thing I am woefully behind on, I am blown away by everything in this novel. The marriage of fantasy and reality, in a very specific Egyptian flavor that feels like I'm being told an age-old myth. The sensation of time being cyclical, generations of the same family going through the same events - or a variation of them - as they grow rich and powerful and then have their dreams crash down due to desires that almost seem inherited from father to son. Mahfouz's writing is impeccable, mixing formal and informal language in a manner that feels real, a snapshot from a time long past. Will be thinking about this masterpiece for a long time.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51165701?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/2181784-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423359" href="/book/show/5502.The_Harafish">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51165701" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8877971755689067489">The Harafish begins with the story of Ashur al-Nagi, an abandoned baby who grows up to bring justice to his Cairo alley as clan chief. Rather than use his position to enrich himself as other chiefs do, Ashur raises up the harafish--or the common people--of the alley while continuing his simple life as a cart driver.<br/><br/>It's also the story of many of his decendants, who aspire to live up to his legendary greatness (greatness through modesty, that is) but mostly fail due to greed, ambition and sometim</span>
  <span id="freeText8877971755689067489" style="display:none">The Harafish begins with the story of Ashur al-Nagi, an abandoned baby who grows up to bring justice to his Cairo alley as clan chief. Rather than use his position to enrich himself as other chiefs do, Ashur raises up the harafish--or the common people--of the alley while continuing his simple life as a cart driver.<br /><br />It's also the story of many of his decendants, who aspire to live up to his legendary greatness (greatness through modesty, that is) but mostly fail due to greed, ambition and sometimes madness. It's about so many decendents, in fact, that I wish I'd drawn a diagram to keep track of all the generations and characters. Several of them have the same names,too. <br /><br />This is truly an epic story, even for Mahfouz. These are good to great short stories individually. Together they chronicle hundreds of years of events in this one community. Though I can't be sure how many hundred without that diagram. Being able to cover this much time in a single book has an interesting effect, but I didn't enjoy it as much as the Cairo Trilogy because the characters don't have as much depth. It's hard to get to know somebody when he goes from fetus to old man in the course of a couple chapters.<br /><br />But I suspect the book is really more about the harafish as a group and their relationship to their leaders. The story really seems to be about legends, heros, tyrants and whether ordinarly people wait for great leaders to bring them justice or decide to seize it for themselves.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8877971755689067489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176148807" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/82840-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465057364p2/82840.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176148807?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/82840-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423359" href="/book/show/5502.The_Harafish">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176148807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16665906091362462660">I tried to read this years ago and couldn't get into it, but when I read it again now, I was hooked. The story moves fast, as in decades fly by with one sentence. It is the epic story of a family descended from a hero, Ashur, who stars in the first story. The subsequent nine stories detail the various downfalls and wild tales of the nine or so (I lost count) generations after him. Like Garcia Marquez's "One Hundred Years of Solitude," this story has a mythic and surreal feel to it. Unlike Garcia</span>
  <span id="freeText16665906091362462660" style="display:none">I tried to read this years ago and couldn't get into it, but when I read it again now, I was hooked. The story moves fast, as in decades fly by with one sentence. It is the epic story of a family descended from a hero, Ashur, who stars in the first story. The subsequent nine stories detail the various downfalls and wild tales of the nine or so (I lost count) generations after him. Like Garcia Marquez's "One Hundred Years of Solitude," this story has a mythic and surreal feel to it. Unlike Garcia Marquez, however, Mahfouz's story is stripped bare of sensuality, yet it is anything but dry. I usually like books with more sumptuously fleshed-out characters, but here, the stark simplicity of the descriptions and Mahfouz's ability to move from one moment in a story to the next with such flawless agility were amazing and compelling. <br /><br />I kept thinking about how to work this book into a study of stories about gangs, because these stories are all about politics and power as various families vie for the coveted position of clan chief. If you read this book, I recommend trying to get through each story in one (or not more than 2) sittings, because, after a while, there are so many characters to keep track of. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16665906091362462660" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3410755186" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3410755186" />
    <a title="Jennifer Jang" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/98419112-jennifer-jang"><img alt="Jennifer Jang" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3410755186?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer Jang" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer Jang" href="/user/show/98419112-jennifer-jang">Jennifer Jang</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423359" href="/book/show/5502.The_Harafish">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3410755186" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11561914494620941808">The Harafish is an eternal loop of death, exile, corruption, despair, and idolatry in various forms. Much like a tv drama, The Harafish provides good entertainment, yet it pales in comparison with The Cairo Trilogy.<br/><br/>The fast pacing of the book doesn't allow one to really connect with any of the characters. They sort of blend into each other, egoistic, hypocritical, and arrogant characters, whose lives are marked only by a series of events (someone's death, marriage, getting seduced, starting to d</span>
  <span id="freeText11561914494620941808" style="display:none">The Harafish is an eternal loop of death, exile, corruption, despair, and idolatry in various forms. Much like a tv drama, The Harafish provides good entertainment, yet it pales in comparison with The Cairo Trilogy.<br /><br />The fast pacing of the book doesn't allow one to really connect with any of the characters. They sort of blend into each other, egoistic, hypocritical, and arrogant characters, whose lives are marked only by a series of events (someone's death, marriage, getting seduced, starting to drink etc) instead of narrated by their thoughts and feelings. The characters' attitudes towards sex are hypocritical - when a man wants another woman, "it's God's will," but when a woman likes another man other than her husband, she's a whore - and she gets killed in one way or another. The characters are impersonal and replaceable, and they seem to feel the same way about each other too. Most depicted relationships are antagonistic. The only humble man in the book is Abd Rabbihi the baker. What happened to nuanced, philosophy-touting characters like Kamal in The Cairo trilogy? What happened to real life dialogues, sibling squabbles, or genuine fondness?<br /><br />The plot for the Harafish was quite predictable too - what happened to Mahalabiyya and Samaha for example, or Zahira, or the last Ashur. Half of the characters either disappear or die out of the blue. Pulling a 1789 near the end is also quite cliche and predictable. I did enjoy reading the Harafish, but it employs a detached, mythical narrative at the expense of developed characters. It doesn't occupy my thoughts like Mahfouz's realist writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11561914494620941808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Parikhit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Parikhit" href="/user/show/4401637-parikhit">Parikhit</a>
      </span>

        rated it

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423359" href="/book/show/5502.The_Harafish">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2311196072" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1380981986680367982">Naguib Mahfouz, without a doubt, is an author I admire for his observation and philosophy. I mustn't be comparing him with authors of the yore but I cannot help draw a resemblance between Mafouz, Dostoyevsky and Charles Dickens; unlike the latter authors Mahfouz keeps his descriptions short, does not ramble much but ensures an effect so profound that you find yourself drawn into the whirlwinds of despondency and questioning the very cores of life as we know it. <br/><br/>'The Harafish' was no different. I</span>
  <span id="freeText1380981986680367982" style="display:none">Naguib Mahfouz, without a doubt, is an author I admire for his observation and philosophy. I mustn't be comparing him with authors of the yore but I cannot help draw a resemblance between Mafouz, Dostoyevsky and Charles Dickens; unlike the latter authors Mahfouz keeps his descriptions short, does not ramble much but ensures an effect so profound that you find yourself drawn into the whirlwinds of despondency and questioning the very cores of life as we know it. <br /><br />'The Harafish' was no different. It is the tale of generations that lived and continue to do so in an alley in Cairo, it is the tale of honour, of greed, of good deeds and bad, of spite, of revenge, of deceit, of redemption. Ashur al-Nagi is an honourable man who becomes the legendary chief of his alley owing his goodness and humanitarian causes. However, he does fall prey to the follies that make us men but redeems himself to create a legacy that is equally loved and despised by the harafish, the bourgeois. His descendants in an attempt to recreate his legacy rise and fail and fail and rise. Some fade away into the oblivion while some etch their goodness into the hearts of the harafish. <br /><br />Likely the entire essence of the story is the vagaries of human emotions, what governs our behaviour, the way we act, and what makes us human. I only wish the book were longer. The entire tale of so many generations in summarised in mere 400 pages! </span>
  <a data-text-id="1380981986680367982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ytt4VFEqaGh8KYsDxMSXtgWyiXbYN0Nzma/YvX++d45Y/RGG6nShANsCP2IzMZ1p0+1uvha6zRTC0Z4Msv2FFA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('05T90KS7Ik164wp3F/QJwxURMKPcvyP5VkeWaKgPyx3pspQCH+XrJd3IvhbgAQMcw07XaxIyrZ4NOdDZZUw5hw==')})
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer6341947827459439276">Naguib Mahfouz (Arabic author profile: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4273240._' rel='nofollow noopener'>نجيب محفوظ</a>) was an Egyptian writer who won the 1988 Nobel Prize for Literature. He published over 50 novels, over 350 short stories, dozens of movie scripts, and five plays over a 70-year career. Many of his works have been made into Egyptian and foreign films.</span>
  <span id="freeText6341947827459439276" style="display:none">Naguib Mahfouz (Arabic author profile: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4273240._" rel="nofollow noopener">نجيب محفوظ</a>) was an Egyptian writer who won the 1988 Nobel Prize for Literature. He published over 50 novels, over 350 short stories, dozens of movie scripts, and five plays over a 70-year career. Many of his works have been made into Egyptian and foreign films.</span>
